  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_981271048" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/981271048" />
    <a title="Caz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4368838-caz"><img alt="Caz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332962126p2/4368838.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/981271048?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caz" href="/user/show/4368838-caz">Caz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781843798590" href="/book/show/22602795-venetia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4368838-caz?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4368838-caz?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4368838-caz?shelf=romance-regency">romance-regency</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4368838-caz?shelf=romance-1800-1850">romance-1800-1850</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer981271048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3113378103757024863"><b>Venetia</b> is, without question, my favourite of all Georgette Heyer’s Regency Romances, and I’ve been looking forward to this new, unabridged audio version for some time. Much of the story takes place in the Autumn, and I’ve always felt that the book has a corresponding maturity about it, an almost elegiac feel in terms of the beauty of the prose and in the depth of the characterisation. Damerel is certainly one of Ms Heyer’s most strongly drawn – and sexiest – heroes, and the eponymous heroine is</span>
  <span id="freeText3113378103757024863" style="display:none"><b>Venetia</b> is, without question, my favourite of all Georgette Heyer’s Regency Romances, and I’ve been looking forward to this new, unabridged audio version for some time. Much of the story takes place in the Autumn, and I’ve always felt that the book has a corresponding maturity about it, an almost elegiac feel in terms of the beauty of the prose and in the depth of the characterisation. Damerel is certainly one of Ms Heyer’s most strongly drawn – and sexiest – heroes, and the eponymous heroine is an absolute gem; intelligent, loving, practical and totally devoid of artifice.<br /><br />Ms Heyer more or less invented the Regency Romance, and authors continue to be inspired to emulate her tales of young bucks and lovely ladies as they navigate their way through the conventions of society and the glittering ballrooms of the <i>ton</i>. <b>Venetia</b> is pretty much the blueprint for the tale of the jaded rake who is reformed by his love for a refreshingly open-hearted young woman, and even though it’s more than half a century old (it was originally published in 1958) it’s still one of the best of its kind.<br /><br />Venetia Lanyon is twenty-five and lives in Yorkshire with her younger brother, Aubrey, who, at seventeen is already a prodigiously talented scholar. Their older brother is away in the army and assumed his father’s baronetcy upon the latter’s death a short time before the book begins. The children lost their mother shortly after Aubrey’s birth, and their reclusive father selfishly isolated his children as well as himself, so that neither Venetia nor Aubrey has much experience of society, and Venetia was never presented at court as was her due as the daughter of a member of the nobility.<br /><br />When walking out one day, she is accosted and “ruthlessly kissed” by a man she has never met, who turns out to be her neighbour, the rakish and disreputable Lord Damerel. Their initial meeting is one of the many delights to be found in this book. Venetia is not missish or prudish; she doesn’t faint, run away, slap him or scold him, instead she stands her ground, flings as many quotations back at him as he throws at her and ends up laughing with him:<br /><br />“Who are you?” he demanded abruptly. “I took you for a village maiden—probably one of my tenants.”<br /><br />“Did you indeed? Well, if that is the way you mean to conduct yourself amongst the village maidens you won’t win much liking here!”<br /><br />“No, no, the danger is that I might win too much!” he retorted. “Who are you? Or should I first present myself to you? I’m Damerel, you know.”<br /><br />“Yes, so I supposed, at the outset of our delightful acquaintance. Later, of course, I was sure of it.”<br /><br />‘Oh, oh—! <i>My reputation, Iago, my reputation!</i>” he exclaimed, laughing again. “Fair Fatality, you are the most unusual female I have encountered in all my thirty-eight years!”<br /><br />“Yon can’t think how deeply flattered I am!” she assured him. “I daresay my head would be quite turned if I didn’t suspect that amongst so many a dozen or so may have slipped from your memory.”<br /><br />Their conversation continues in a like manner, and even at this early stage of the book, it’s clear that theirs has been a true meeting of minds and spirits. Both are more than a little smitten, and when they are thrown into each other’s company following an accident, they are able to spend time together which leads to the blossoming of a tender and deeply affectionate friendship.<br /><br />If ever there was a couple in a romance that deserved to be called “soul mates”, it is Venetia and Damerel. He is charmed by her complete lack of affectation, her vivacity and wonderful sense of humour. He may be a rake, but he is also clever, intuitive, funny and kind, opening up a whole new world for Venetia by filling a need she’d never before realised:<br /><br />“Why, oh <i>why</i> did I never know you until now?”<br /><br />“It does seem a pity,” she agreed. “I have been thinking so myself, for I always wished for a friend to laugh with.”<br /><br />“To laugh with,” he repeated slowly.<br /><br />“Perhaps you have friends already who laugh when you do,” she said diffidently. “I haven’t, and it’s important, I think—more important than sympathy in affliction, which you might easily find in someone you positively disliked.”<br /><br />“But to share a sense of the ridiculous prohibits dislike—yes, that’s true. And rare! My God, how rare! Do they stare at you, our worthy neighbours, when you laugh?”<br /><br />“Yes! or ask me what I mean when I’m joking!”<br /><br />But Damerel has seen much more of the world than Venetia has, and knows the damage her association with him could cause to her reputation. So when he realises that he’s in over his head as far as she is concerned and that what he had originally intended as no more than a pleasant diversion has turned into something much more, he tries to distance himself from her in a scene that, even though I’ve read it several times, continues to bring a lump to my throat. Fortunately for both of them, Venetia is not going to let love slip away so easily.<br /><br />Damerel often refers to Venetia as his “dear delight”, and that’s often how I think of this book – a dear delight. It abounds with literary allusion; the writing sparkles and the interplay between the leads has rarely – if ever – been bettered, either by Ms Heyer or any other author since. The romance is simply beautiful and although the book is squeaky clean, it possesses a sensuality not often found in the author’s work.<br /><br />This new audio version of <b>Venetia</b> is also a complete delight. Phyllida Nash has already recorded a number of Ms Heyer’s works, so I was pleased when I learned that she was to narrate this, as I knew my favourite story would be in safe hands. She has a deep, mellifluous speaking voice which allows her to voice the male roles comfortably, and her narrative is well paced and beautifully nuanced. Ms Nash has a deft touch with the humour in the story, and is a narrator who “acts” - by which I mean if the text says that a character yawns, they yawn, or if they say something “with a laugh”, then the laugh is present in a naturalistic manner. All the characters are well differentiated by use of a variety of tone and accent so that there is never any confusion as to who is speaking. The two principals are just as expertly portrayed, with Damerel being particularly well characterised and sounding exactly as he should – authoritative, knowledgeable and rather sexy.<br /><br />I’ve been waiting for an unabridged audio version of <b>Venetia</b> to come along for years, and while the wait has been frustrating, it has also undoubtedly been worth it. Regardless of whether you’re a fan of this particular story, a Heyer fan in general, or if you’ve never read or listened to one of her books before, this is an audio experience that’s sure to enchant.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3113378103757024863" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1311406361?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marquise" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marquise" href="/user/show/4674014-marquise">Marquise</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781402238840" href="/book/show/10051392-venetia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=the-things-i-read-for-lymond">the-things-i-read-for-lymond</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=best-loved-reads">best-loved-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=historical-romance">historical-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=historical-setting-1800s">historical-setting-1800s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=5-stars">5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4674014-marquise?shelf=regency-britain">regency-britain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1311406361" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17849748753003415282"><i>Je n'aurais jamais cru que Monseigneur le Duc d'Avon pourrait être remplacé par un autre héros!</i><br/><br/>When I start to mutter in French, it hints the book must've made an impression. And, dear me, this one did. How could it not? It's very intense for the average Heyer novel, a writer that normally shies away from overt sexiness as a scalded cat from water. A good timing it had, too, for I was on the edge of losing faith in this author's capacity to create masculine characters as interesting as Monseigne</span>
  <span id="freeText17849748753003415282" style="display:none"><i>Je n'aurais jamais cru que Monseigneur le Duc d'Avon pourrait être remplacé par un autre héros!</i><br /><br />When I start to mutter in French, it hints the book must've made an impression. And, dear me, this one did. How could it not? It's very intense for the average Heyer novel, a writer that normally shies away from overt sexiness as a scalded cat from water. A good timing it had, too, for I was on the edge of losing faith in this author's capacity to create masculine characters as interesting as Monseigneur and heroines that weren't behaving like <i>stoopid</i> ten-year-old schoolgirls at the ripe old age of, say, twenty.<br /><br />But that happened. Enter Jasper, Lord Damerel, aptly described by this line from a poem by another famous rake of the same period, Lord Byron, that our hero quotes:<br /><br /><b>
  <blockquote>"There was a laughing devil in his sneer,<br />That raised emotions both of rage and fear;<br />And where his frown of hatred darkly fell,<br />Hope withering fled, and Mercy sighed farewell!"</blockquote>
</b><br />The way he and Venetia, the best Heyer heroine I've found so far, met is one of the funniest rendezvous I've read in historical romance. That scene of Shakespearean inspiration establishes that Damerel is a jaded rake in semi-retirement that sets out to tease what he thinks is a silly pretty country girl he'll soon forget, but instead finds someone that'll be his best friend and his match. She, who believes herself fit only for spinterhood and taking care of and keeping house for her crippled younger brother, is a very resourceful, optimistic and persistent girl with wits that allow her to keep up with Damerel's quotin' &amp; recitin', and, veering away from the usual plot that has the hero pursue the heroine, it's <i>she</i> who is in charge and has to manoeuvre her way towards attaining her wishes, to her benefit and her brother's. Their relationship builds up at quite an slow pace, and they don't get together until much later, but that doesn't feel like a negative precisely, because they're "together" all the time in the sense that they interact on an almost daily basis, and reading their meetings described, it's possible to witness the growing affection so that the resolution feels a natural conclusion. This is one aspect I particularly like, because sometimes authors make the mistake of bringing a couple together in the last chapter with little buildup and too much conflict thrown in that it is blindingly obvious that it's just for drama. Here, the obstacles aren't the dreaded Big Misunderstanding, but come from social conventions and characterisation, and, I think, are solved as the plot moves. <br /><br />All is set in the country, save for a brief time in London (which didn't include the "ton," gods be praised!), but not quiet-paced as I'd believed it would as soon as I discovered its setting, it's rather eventful for the narrow scope, thanks to the neighbouring families and Damerel himself, plus Venetia's brother is a strong secondary character. Even the unavoidable pest that populates Heyer's novels brings in some necessary conflict to the narrative, although I wish it hadn't been dropped because the Scorrier/Lady Lanyon plot was, I feel, left without resolution, for good or for bad. You just cannot include an antagonist and then leave them forgotten somewhere. And that, together with the way the Lanyons react to <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[their mother's secret past being discovered <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, is the weakest part of the story. It was simply unconvincing that they'd react with so much equanimity given what the lady did and how it affected their lives, and more of that cool anger shown by Venetia should've been in.<br /><br />I definitely am going to approach the rest of her novels with more hopes about Heyer's writing after this enjoyable read that is going straight to my favourites and after such delightful characters, both male <i>and</i> female (Did I thank the gods already?). Oh, and I give the edge to Damerel on account of his ability to recite all poets worth their salt at appropriate times! </span>
  <a data-text-id="17849748753003415282" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2835442159" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2835442159" />
    <a title="Abigail Bok" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32345761-abigail-bok"><img alt="Abigail Bok" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442980475p2/32345761.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2835442159?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abigail Bok" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abigail Bok" href="/user/show/32345761-abigail-bok">Abigail Bok</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2835442159" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer733673054398092580"><i>Venetia</i> is many readers’ favorite Heyer novel, but not mine. The heroine is older, bolder, and little concerned with the proprieties; she is also kind and thoughtful and beautiful, so you get to have it both ways. The hero is the epitome of Heyer’s rakish strain of male leads, and he comes to Venetia’s neighborhood trailing epigrams and the sulfurous odor of wickedness. They meet and sparks instantaneously fly, but his reputation and secrets from her past generate lots of opposition. The hero, t</span>
  <span id="freeText733673054398092580" style="display:none"><i>Venetia</i> is many readers’ favorite Heyer novel, but not mine. The heroine is older, bolder, and little concerned with the proprieties; she is also kind and thoughtful and beautiful, so you get to have it both ways. The hero is the epitome of Heyer’s rakish strain of male leads, and he comes to Venetia’s neighborhood trailing epigrams and the sulfurous odor of wickedness. They meet and sparks instantaneously fly, but his reputation and secrets from her past generate lots of opposition. The hero, trying to reform and be belatedly (and, it must be said, inconsistently) noble, tries to renounce the heroine, but the heart knows what it knows and a happy ending is guaranteed.<br /><br />It’s a challenge for a feminist to manage the cognitive dissonance of enjoying romantic fiction, and I believe the root of my dislike for this book lies in the fact that here, the dissonance is not a whisper but a roar. This freethinking heroine is a freethinker only in a 1950s kind of way (the decade when the book was written; it’s set around 1818) that I find particularly toxic. Example: early in the story, when contemplating the life of a rake, our heroine’s thoughts run thus: “With his loves she was as little concerned as with his first encounter with herself [when he seized her, a total stranger, and forcibly kissed her]. That had angered her, but it had neither shocked nor disgusted her. Men—witness all the histories!—were subject to sudden lusts and violences, affairs that seemed strangely divorced from heart or head, and often more strangely still from what were surely their true characters. For them chastity was not a prime virtue: she remembered her amazement when she had discovered that so correct a gentleman and kind a husband as Sir John Denny had not always been faithful to his lady. Had Lady Denny cared? A little, perhaps, but she had not allowed it to blight her marriage.” She eagerly sops up advice like “Never seek to pry into what does not concern you, but rather look in the opposite direction!” (I suppose the wife can just look the other way when she develops syphilis?) In my worldview this is cheating, a profound betrayal, and the wife who accepts it is an enabler. There is no earthly reason for the perpetuation of such a blatant double standard. Men can be as violent and dishonest as they please, because it’s simply their nature? So much for any romantic feelz I might have been building up; I have no desire to imagine myself in her shoes.<br /><br />That central premise of this book is what most irritates me, but there are other more minor annoyances. It is more explicitly sensual and romantic than most of Heyer’s novels, and I am more attracted to restraint than to overt accounts of emotion and desire. Venetia is a physical being, and her love for the hero is in the reader’s face on every page—for me, that ruins the suspense and leaves me no gaps to fill in with my own imagination. To underscore the couple’s intense attachment, Heyer puts endless epithets in their mouths like “my dear friend” and “my lovely one” and has the heroine lift her face for a kiss (from a man who has not declared his intentions) multiple times. According to the manners of the age, this behavior would make her a strumpet, and I’m embarrassed to be around two people with so little conduct. These are, I suspect, the very qualities of the book that appeal to many readers, so let us simply say it’s not to my taste.<br /><br />I also find it uneven in a way that Heyer rarely is. Characters occasionally do things that are out of character. The antagonists are caricatures of their types, overdrawn and one-note. And the resolution comes suddenly, in the middle of a scene, when the hero reverses a strongly held position without meaningful explanation. I’ve now given this book every chance I could, read it three times, and life’s too short for any further atttempts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="733673054398092580" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1641883690" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="ᴥ Irena ᴥ" href="/user/show/5252054-irena">ᴥ Irena ᴥ</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/12282337-venetia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5252054-irena?shelf=historical-romance">historical-romance</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1641883690" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3804133613761821583"><b>3.5</b><br/><br/>While reading <i>Venetia</i> I spent half of my time being thrilled (Lord Damerel and Venetia are both wonderful characters) and the other half being frustrated, annoyed and plain angry. There was no middle, people.<br/><br/>On the one hand, you have these two wonderful people. Lord Damerel is said to be every horrible and ugly thing under the sun, but to me he was perfect. He never does anything bad in the book. His actions speak louder than any pretty words could. Although his first appearance isn't exactly</span>
  <span id="freeText3804133613761821583" style="display:none"><b>3.5</b><br /><br />While reading <i>Venetia</i> I spent half of my time being thrilled (Lord Damerel and Venetia are both wonderful characters) and the other half being frustrated, annoyed and plain angry. There was no middle, people.<br /><br />On the one hand, you have these two wonderful people. Lord Damerel is said to be every horrible and ugly thing under the sun, but to me he was perfect. He never does anything bad in the book. His actions speak louder than any pretty words could. Although his first appearance isn't exactly something to praise, he could be one of my favourite characters so far. Yes, he had depleted his own fortune but, as he said, they will live comfortably. <br />I loved Venetia too. She is so refreshingly honest without being too naive or stupid. A couple of times I wanted her to be more rude (some people more than deserved it), but she never allowed those situations to go too far. The one time I needed her to speak up and put the horrible people where they belong, she did just that.<br /><br />On the other hand, Venetia's meddling family and friends were a bit too much. I hated Edward and the way he acted with her, as if she doesn't know her own mind. Speaking of all those annoying meddling people, I must say I loved every Aubrey moment. Venetia's younger brother was great. Whenever someone needed a put-down, Aubrey was perfect for the role. <br /><br />Overall, an enjoyable, although a bit exaggerated, story with two wonderful main characters.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2025054132" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2025054132" />
    <a title="Sammy Loves Books" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14227114-sammy-loves-books"><img alt="Sammy Loves Books" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534119573p2/14227114.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2025054132?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sammy Loves Books" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sammy Loves Books" href="/user/show/14227114-sammy-loves-books">Sammy Loves Books</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/22675634-venetia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14227114-sammy-loves-books?shelf=he-owns-my-heart">he-owns-my-heart</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14227114-sammy-loves-books?shelf=tear-jerker">tear-jerker</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14227114-sammy-loves-books?shelf=lmao-funny">lmao-funny</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14227114-sammy-loves-books?shelf=library-audio">library-audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14227114-sammy-loves-books?shelf=best-books-ever">best-books-ever</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14227114-sammy-loves-books?shelf=historical-romance">historical-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14227114-sammy-loves-books?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

<div id="review_666834980" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/666834980" />
    <a title="QNPoohBear" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22177907-qnpoohbear"><img alt="QNPoohBear" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527904848p2/22177907.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/666834980?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="QNPoohBear" class="user" itemprop="url" name="QNPoohBear" href="/user/show/22177907-qnpoohbear">QNPoohBear</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22177907-qnpoohbear?shelf=clean-romance">clean-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22177907-qnpoohbear?shelf=regency-romance">regency-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22177907-qnpoohbear?shelf=kisses-only">kisses-only</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22177907-qnpoohbear?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer666834980" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6551359785738745692">At five-and-twenty Venetia Lanyon has never been in love. All her experience with romance comes from between the covers of a book. All her experience of the world outside her Yorkshire village comes from the books she has read. Yet, she does not dream of romance and is content to run her family's estate while her eldest brother is with the Army of Occupation on the Continent. She enjoys the company of her younger brother Aubrey and when Conway returns home and Aubrey goes to University, Venetia </span>
  <span id="freeText6551359785738745692" style="display:none">At five-and-twenty Venetia Lanyon has never been in love. All her experience with romance comes from between the covers of a book. All her experience of the world outside her Yorkshire village comes from the books she has read. Yet, she does not dream of romance and is content to run her family's estate while her eldest brother is with the Army of Occupation on the Continent. She enjoys the company of her younger brother Aubrey and when Conway returns home and Aubrey goes to University, Venetia plans to set up house for herself and Aubrey. Marrying her neighbor Edward Yardley does not factor into her plans, despite what Edward may think. While out berry picking one afternoon, Venetia runs into the "Wicked Baron", Lord Damerel, her nearest neighbor and the object of village gossip. She's been told that Lord Damerel is a wicked libertine who, on his on his most recent visit, held an "orgy" (wild drunken party with gentlemen and women of loose morals). When Damerel steals a kiss from pretty Venetia, she does not scream or swoon. Instead, she responds with an angry retort and witty quotations from her favorite books. When Aubrey meets with an accident that forces him to recover at Damerel's home, Venetia and Lord Damarel discover an easy friendship that comes from a shared love of literature and the same sense of humor. Even after Aubrey returns home, Damerel remains in Yorkshire long after he was expected to return to London. The so-called Wicked Baron earns the enmity of both Edward and Venetia's much younger suitor, Oswald Denny. Then comes a day when Venetia is forced to think about her future and what she wants for her future happiness. Damerel is forced to confront his own feelings and finds that his original plan has gone awry. What will their fates be?<br /><br />This is my top Georgette Heyer and one of my top Regency books. I love everything about this book, especially the characters. First we have the unconventional heroine, Venetia, who is neither young nor silly. She's sensible, has a pragmatic nature and a wicked sense of humor. She shares her sense of the absurd with Lord Damerel, which is why I think they make the perfect couple. They fell into friendship first based on mutual interests and personality type. Then they fell into love. Georgette Heyer was more concerned with relationships of the mind "true meeting of like minds" than with passion but this book also sizzles with unstated passion. You can feel the bond between the heroine and hero as their relationship develops. <br /><br />Many people dislike Damerel and dismiss him based on his reputation alone. This is true for readers as well as characters in the book. If you read the novel closely, you will know that Damerel is not an unrepentant rake or libertine. If he was a libertine, he would have seduced Venetia and been done with it. If he was unrepentant, he would have gone back to London or to his friend's hunting box to carouse. If the reader pays attention, they will note that Damerel's man, Marston notes that his master only drinks to excess when he's bored or really upset. In fact his whole career as a rake began with ONE mistake in his youth. He got carried away with a youthful passion and then was labeled black by all who knew of him. He then proceeded to live up to his reputation because why not? If everyone thinks him so bad then so be it! However, in the few months the story takes place we get to know him quite well and understand that he's sensitive about his wicked reputation. He feels deeply that his family was prepared to write him off. Yes the first time he met Venetia he behaved very badly, but the way it's written we know it's supposed to be funny, especially when Venetia does not behave as a typical heroine in a romance. Her behavior intrigues and attracts Damerel but it takes Aubrey's accident to really bring them together. The ONLY time he gets drunk after the initial "meet cute" is towards the end when he's suffering. If he was an unrepentant rake, he would be drunk a lot more and not care. He wouldn't have been so good with Aubrey or had such a great time exchanging witty banter with Venetia. I think Damerel must be based on Rhett Butler or Rhett based on a GH hero! I disliked Rhett in the movie because he did drink to excess at times and he did not share a true connection with Scarlett (who I hated even more). <br /><br />Some readers take exception to the "boys will be boys" attitude of the novel but you must remember that the story takes place in 1818 and was written in 1958. Neither time was a great time for women and in the context of marriage at that time, it makes perfect sense. People married for money and power. If a husband and wife had some degree of affection for one another, they were lucky. A wife was supposed to turn a blind eye to her husband's extramarital activities. "Thou shall be discreet" was the cardinal rule of relationships. To be fair, many women also had affairs. At the end of the novel, Damerel is honest about not sure if he can entirely reform and Venetia is fine with that. For one thing, she's bored. Also, she loves him enough to know that his love for her will never change no matter what he does. I think he can be faithful because he'll never be bored with such a good friend by his side and he'll be busy dealing with her brothers and their problems. The relationship between Lord and Lady Steele is nice because he flatters her vanity and she allows him to flirt. They accept one another for who they are and Venetia knows that. She accepts Damerel for who he is and unlike Edward, he appreciates her true self. Thus I think they will have a long, happy marriage. <br /><br />The plot develops well until Ch. 11 when it reaches a painful turning point. The actions from there until Ch. 20 are difficult to read. I always have a hard time with this section of the novel. Georgette Heyer once again creates quirky secondary characters and Mrs. Scurrier is one of the best. I love to hate her. The plot winds up a little too quickly and needs a bit more at the end. <br /><br />The plot and the characters truly make this one of Georgette Heyer's best among her many masterpieces. </span>
  <a data-text-id="6551359785738745692" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_133541905" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/133541905" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4542979-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454402751p2/4542979.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/133541905?book_show_action=true">Dec 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/4542979-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/7422313-venetia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=all-time-favourites">all-time-favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4542979-kim?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer133541905" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1031302347785091322">I started reading Georgette Heyer when I was a teenager, some thirty five years ago, when my mother gave me Friday's Child and told me she thought I would enjoy it. Since then I have read all the romances, a number of them countless times. They have long been the books I turn to when I'm feeling unwell, a bit fragile, or when it's cold and wet and I need a comfort read! <br/><br/>Just recently I have started listening to audiobook versions of Heyer novels. I did not think that I would enjoy listening to b</span>
  <span id="freeText1031302347785091322" style="display:none">I started reading Georgette Heyer when I was a teenager, some thirty five years ago, when my mother gave me Friday's Child and told me she thought I would enjoy it. Since then I have read all the romances, a number of them countless times. They have long been the books I turn to when I'm feeling unwell, a bit fragile, or when it's cold and wet and I need a comfort read! <br /><br />Just recently I have started listening to audiobook versions of Heyer novels. I did not think that I would enjoy listening to book readings, but I have been acquiring audiobooks to give to my mother, who has lost her sight and have greatly enjoyed listening to them myself. This brings me to Venetia. I still have my first copy of Venetia, a 1971 paperback edition, now showing signs of multiple re-reads. I am currently listening to the unabridged audiobook read by Sian Phillips. (There is an abridged version available and as much as I appreciate the narrator, Richard Armitage, I am sure I would get annoyed listening to an abridged version of such a well-loved book.) <br /><br />I realized this afternoon, as I listened, that I always feel tears come to my eyes at exactly the same place in the narrative. Feeling weepy is not my usual reaction to reading Heyer. Indeed, off the top of my head, I think that the only other one of her books to have that effect on me is Sylvester, in which a scene towards the end of the book never fails to bring a lump to my throat. Venetia may not be great literature, but in my view it has quiet emotional power, great sweetness and an engaging narrative. It is a book that I have probably read upwards of a dozen times over the years. I am sure that I will read it (or listen to it) many more times in years to come. Venetia is one of Heyer's most likeable heroines and Damerel is one of her most attractive heroes. The minor characters are interesting and even Flurry the dog is beautifully realized! Venetia is definitely on my Top Five Heyer list. Today it's my absolute favourite, but I suspect that's only because it's the one that's engaging me right now! <br /><br />Update: 5 October 2011. I have just finished a re-read of this novel, as a buddy read with my friend Jemidar. This time I read it on kindle, to save my 1971 paperback edition from further wear and tear. It remains as special to me as ever, my pavlovian response of tearing up in the middle of chapter 15 quite unchanged. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1031302347785091322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1837520722" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1837520722" />
    <a title="Emily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2431873-emily"><img alt="Emily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1477433724p2/2431873.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1837520722?book_show_action=true">Dec 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily" href="/user/show/2431873-emily">Emily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11432520-venetia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2431873-emily?shelf=regency-era">regency-era</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2431873-emily?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2431873-emily?shelf=favorites-2017">favorites-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2431873-emily?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1837520722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4674463840157910297">I LOVED this book. It's definitely my favorite Heyer and probably my favorite regency romance. The entire book made me laugh, from descriptions of Oswald Denny attempting to become the <i>Corsair</i> to the many delightful exchanges between Damerel and Venetia, who are a couple that are truly fun to read about. Every character is fully realized, from the odious Edward Yardley (someone I wish I could have shaken!) to the self-absorbed Lady Steeple. This world feels real, and firmly grounded in the right</span>
  <span id="freeText4674463840157910297" style="display:none">I LOVED this book. It's definitely my favorite Heyer and probably my favorite regency romance. The entire book made me laugh, from descriptions of Oswald Denny attempting to become the <i>Corsair</i> to the many delightful exchanges between Damerel and Venetia, who are a couple that are truly fun to read about. Every character is fully realized, from the odious Edward Yardley (someone I wish I could have shaken!) to the self-absorbed Lady Steeple. This world feels real, and firmly grounded in the right era.<br /><br />Heyer is the gold standard of regency romance because she doesn't take modern characters and put them onto a flimsy background: she actually writes characters and plots that take place in 1816 England. Venetia is an unusual girl (practically on the shelf, at the advanced age of five-and-twenty), but she's firmly planted in the era and isn't an anachronistic heroine with modern ideas. Heyer's ability to write characters who are slightly outside of the norm, but who aren't marching around demanding the vote, makes her books fun to read. And her knowledge of regency fashion is unparalleled:<br /><br /><blockquote>The silly pigeon rigged himself out as fine as fivepence, and trotted round to Grosvenor Square looking precise to a pin: Inexpressibles of the most delicate shade of primrose, coat by Stultz, Hessians by Hoby, hat – the Bang-up – by Baxter, neckcloth – the Oriental, which is remarkable for its height – by himself. Add to all this a Barcelona handkerchief, a buttonhole as large as a cabbage, a strong aroma of Circassian hair-oil, the deportment of a dancing-master, and a lisp it took him years to bring to perfection, and you will perceive that Alfred is not just in the ordinary style!</blockquote><br />I love Venetia, I love Damerel, I love Aubrey, I love every impulse that wars inside Oswald Denny's breast, and I really, really enjoyed this book. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4674463840157910297" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_23215119" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/23215119?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kelly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kelly" href="/user/show/94602-kelly">Kelly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=regency">regency</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=owned">owned</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=its-the-quiet-ones">its-the-quiet-ones</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/94602-kelly?shelf=brit-lit">brit-lit</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer23215119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3681938443327832955">Its said that Georgette Heyer created the Regency romance genre, and future authors used her books as their model. It is my opinion that they used this specific book as a model. At least, the good ones did. This particular book felt much more modern than the other ones, in the sense that I've seen other authors try to rip off this story and its tone in more recent books many many times. But that's because its very well done and very enjoyable. The romance centers on a friendship rather than a re</span>
  <span id="freeText3681938443327832955" style="display:none">Its said that Georgette Heyer created the Regency romance genre, and future authors used her books as their model. It is my opinion that they used this specific book as a model. At least, the good ones did. This particular book felt much more modern than the other ones, in the sense that I've seen other authors try to rip off this story and its tone in more recent books many many times. But that's because its very well done and very enjoyable. The romance centers on a friendship rather than a real courtship. Its two people, Venetia and her rakish neighbor Lord Demerel, who really enjoy each other's company and are very convincing as friends who eventually realize that they're in love with each other. It doesn't feel contrived in the least. It's not sickeningly romantic, but rather sweet. Also, I love the supporting characters of Venetia's scholarly brother and her crotchety old Nurse. I laughed my way through most of this and finished it in a wonderful, relaxing afternoon. One of Heyer's best, and highly recommended to newcomers to her books (though I still think starting with The Grand Sophy or Cotillion is best).</span>
  <a data-text-id="3681938443327832955" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1376480966" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19611464-seema-khan?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19611464-seema-khan?shelf=favourite">favourite</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19611464-seema-khan?shelf=my-fav-author">my-fav-author</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1376480966" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17736463745724266938">4.85 stars actually.. Why not the complete 5 I shall say presently.<br/>There are those books which you never want to end and which you never want to keep away; Venetia is one such book! I literally felt bad that it ended! And it is one of the rare books in the middle of which I felt like crying! It so touched me! Yet again Georgette Heyer magic is very evident in this book. I am so glad that I did read this one.<br/>Venetia stands very close to Frederica (My No.1 Heyer)in my list of favourites.<br/><br/>Venetia is</span>
  <span id="freeText17736463745724266938" style="display:none">4.85 stars actually.. Why not the complete 5 I shall say presently.<br />There are those books which you never want to end and which you never want to keep away; Venetia is one such book! I literally felt bad that it ended! And it is one of the rare books in the middle of which I felt like crying! It so touched me! Yet again Georgette Heyer magic is very evident in this book. I am so glad that I did read this one.<br />Venetia stands very close to Frederica (My No.1 Heyer)in my list of favourites.<br /><br />Venetia is no doubt my favourite Heyer heroine along side Frederica and Sophy. She is witty, charming, outspoken, kind, and beautiful. She really gives a character to the story, very genuine. Her perseverance through everything and her attitude towards her parents is depicted ingeniously. <br />Lord Damerel is such a rake! And is still so likeable! He is The Anti Hero to look out for! For all his libertine ways he is still adorable and makes it to my favourite hero's list. What I like most about him is his laughing disposition, he really does that a lot and that too through tough situations. Real strength of character. And also he is not making any false promises of reformation, which I find very believable; and it is mentioned by Venetia that she doesn't expect him to change out of his lifelong habits overnight or for that matter, ever! I loved his character and the way he recognizes himself and how he is willing to let Venetia go just so that she doesn't suffer because of his reputation. Quite selfless for a rake! My heart nearly broke at a description of him when Venetia comes back from London to see him sunken beyond identity! <br /><br />About the story. It is brilliant, though I must admit that the beginning gave me quite a 'Frederica feeling' for the similarities it does have. <br />What a beginning to be sure and what an entry of the hero! It had me all-struck and sitting straight saying to myself 'Did he really do that!?!' I mean who kisses anyone one sets eyes on for the first time?? It had me intrigued as to what further can this rake do!! I couldn't stop reading the book once I started!<br />The growth of the story is fascinating and the surprise of Venetia's mother being alive is too good! Also I really find an aspect in the book which not many a pen can achieve in communicating. How Venetia falls in love and is ready to sink in the eyes of the world just for the sake of her love is just very touching. Though both of them never claim it but they really get true love from each other. <br />I liked Aubrey's character as well. Nice and intelligent. Edward was a real pain to bear (In Venetia's behest that is!)Other characters also put in a great deal. But the highlight is Venetia and Damerels chemistry and their relationship.<br />Now why didn't I give the complete 5.. I really wanted to know what happens to Conway's mother in law Mrs Scorrier. She should have been shown to be getting a good set down by Conway who may have returned or at least something like this!<br />Other than this I find the book amazing! #MustRead</span>
  <a data-text-id="17736463745724266938" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_245198173" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Maggie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4222084-maggie"><img alt="Maggie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319666686p2/4222084.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/245198173?book_show_action=true">Dec 09, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maggie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maggie" href="/user/show/4222084-maggie">Maggie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11432520-venetia">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Maggie by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2448124-noelle">Noelle</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222084-maggie?shelf=crushworthy-guys">crushworthy-guys</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222084-maggie?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222084-maggie?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4222084-maggie?shelf=i-ve-just-read-you-and-i-love-you">i-ve-just-read-you-and-i-love-you</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer245198173" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11998903236787287870"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18067.Georgette_Heyer' title='Georgette Heyer' rel='nofollow noopener'>Georgette Heyer</a> is the first author I've read who makes <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1265.Jane_Austen' title='Jane Austen' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jane Austen</a> seem emo. Don't get me wrong, I adore Austen and consider WWJD to stand for "What Would Jane Do?", but I really enjoyed this charming and angst-free Regency tale of Venetia and her Wicked Baron, the <i>rake</i> Damerel. Oh Damerel... Imagine <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14935.Sense_and_Sensibility' title='Sense and Sensibility by Jane Austen' rel='nofollow noopener'>Sense and Sensibility</a>'s Willoughby and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre' title='Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jane Eyre</a>'s Rochester without their respective issues -- or wives. Damerel is charming, mischievous, and funny. The same could also be said of Venetia, who is</span>
  <span id="freeText11998903236787287870" style="display:none"><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18067.Georgette_Heyer" title="Georgette Heyer" rel="nofollow noopener">Georgette Heyer</a> is the first author I've read who makes <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1265.Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Austen</a> seem emo. Don't get me wrong, I adore Austen and consider WWJD to stand for "What Would Jane Do?", but I really enjoyed this charming and angst-free Regency tale of Venetia and her Wicked Baron, the <i>rake</i> Damerel. Oh Damerel... Imagine <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14935.Sense_and_Sensibility" title="Sense and Sensibility by Jane Austen" rel="nofollow noopener">Sense and Sensibility</a>'s Willoughby and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a>'s Rochester without their respective issues -- or wives. Damerel is charming, mischievous, and funny. The same could also be said of Venetia, who is nearing spinsterhood at, gasp!, five-and-twenty.<br><br>Heyer touches on social issues, whereas Austen really delves into them, but don't confuse lightness of touch for lack of deftness. How can else an author get away with mentioning <i>orgies</i> while staying true to the time period? And not just mentioning them, but having her characters joke about them! There is so much humor and laughter throughout the novel, and not because the characters are ridiculous or silly.<br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380364651ra/716413.gif" width="344" height="196" alt="" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>You're laughing along with them, not at them. Okay, maybe you're laughing at too serious Aubrey, who nonetheless ended up being one of my favorite characters.<br><br>I had a hard time letting go of my favorite Austen characters too and turned to terrible-to-fucking-terrible books like <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/110095.An_Assembly_Such_as_This__Fitzwilliam_Darcy__Gentleman__1_" title="An Assembly Such as This (Fitzwilliam Darcy, Gentleman #1) by Pamela Aidan" rel="nofollow noopener">An Assembly Such as This</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/83221.Mr__Darcy_Takes_a_Wife_Pride_and_Prejudice_Continues" title="Mr. Darcy Takes a Wife Pride and Prejudice Continues by Linda Berdoll" rel="nofollow noopener">Mr. Darcy Takes a Wife</a>, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6435899.A_Little_Bit_Psychic_Pride___Prejudice_with_a_Modern_Twist" title="A Little Bit Psychic Pride &amp; Prejudice with a Modern Twist by Aimée Avery" rel="nofollow noopener">A Little Bit Psychic</a>. Spare yourself the ignominy and read a book that doesn't have Austen rolling over in her grave. I highly recommend delightful <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32102.Venetia" title="Venetia by Georgette Heyer" rel="nofollow noopener">Venetia</a>!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11998903236787287870" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2535303413" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2535303413" />
    <a title="Mei" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6876967-mei"><img alt="Mei" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1422277196p2/6876967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2535303413?book_show_action=true">Sep 24, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mei" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mei" href="/user/show/6876967-mei">Mei</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6876967-mei?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6876967-mei?shelf=no-books">no-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2535303413" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12869553560184277664">I’m really sorry, but I didn’t liked it… I suppose the story could have been interesting, but the writing style was too confusing for me.<br/><br/><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>Unfortunately there was no possibility of following it up in the accepted mode, and although several susceptible gentlemen inveighed bitterly against the barbarity of a parent who would permit no visitor to enter his house none of them was so deeply heart-smitten after standing up with the lovely Miss Lanyon for one country-dance as to cast aside every </blockquote></i></b></span>
  <span id="freeText12869553560184277664" style="display:none">I’m really sorry, but I didn’t liked it… I suppose the story could have been interesting, but the writing style was too confusing for me.<br><br><b>
  <i>
    <blockquote>Unfortunately there was no possibility of following it up in the accepted mode, and although several susceptible gentlemen inveighed bitterly against the barbarity of a parent who would permit no visitor to enter his house none of them was so deeply heart-smitten after standing up with the lovely Miss Lanyon for one country-dance as to cast aside every canon of propriety (as well as to the horrid dread of making a great cake of himself) and ride out of York to Undershaw, either to hang about the gates of the Manor in the hope of achieving a clandestine meeting with Venetia, or to force his way into the house.</blockquote>
  </i>
</b><br><br>Maybe the above is the writing style for the period, but I had a tough time getting what the author meant… I had to make a grammar exercise: which is the main sentence? Which is the secondary?<br>Sorry, but when I read I want to be entertained…<br><br>And let’s talk about another strange word? Here a lot of characters… <b>ejeculate</b>!!!! <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1537791692ra/26346419.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The first time I read it, I thought it was an error, but it kept reappearing! <br><br>So, these are the two main reasons, but there’re also others that involve the story itself…<br><br>I couldn’t understand why Venetia couldn’t shake off the idiots in her life: Edward and Oswald. <br><br>Edward is obviously a mama-boy and also somewhat heard of hearing, because the poor Venetia cannot convince him that she doesn’t want him! Sanctimonious idiot!<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537791692i/26346418._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Oswald is a young puppy who just wants what he wants, but at least he was excluded from the story much sooner than Edward.<br><br>And shell we talk about Mrs. Scorrier? A social climber who just want to take Venetia’s place! Rude and nasty, But Venetia doesn’t put her down even when she went and offend her lame brother!<br><br>The problem is that Venetia doesn’t want to hurt the feelings of every person who’s near her, even if they’re making her life miserable! I wanted to yell at her to throw them all out of her life with a good kick on their collective backside!<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537791692i/26346416.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Now about Jasper… he was the supposed villain here that everybody and their neighbours wanted to remove from Venetia’s life. And I don’t get why… There was some very crooked explanation given by her aunt about his being a rake and Venetia being too virtuous for him, but I didn’t understand it at all… Something to do with her age and beauty and his rakish ways… Mah…<br><br>I didn’t find Jasper wicked at all: he was the exact opposite! He was intelligent and nice and friendly. The only one not against him was Venetia’s brother Aubrey.<br><br>Aubrey is the most grey-area character here. He’s very clever, but very young and selfish and doesn’t help Venetia in any way. He’s supposedly supportive of her against Mrs. Scorrier, but doesn’t realize that it would be more helpful is he went away. <br><br>Now, the issue with Venetia’s mother… Gah… Gah for several reasons: a degenerate mother, selfish woman and idiot to boot. But my biggest problem is that everybody, and I stress EVERYBODY, new that she wasn’t dead, but nobody thought to tell that to Venetia!!!!<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1537791692i/26346417._SX540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I’m really, really sorry, but this was not for me…</span>
  <a data-text-id="12869553560184277664" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2626091371" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2626091371" />
    <a title="Mela" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41757430-mela"><img alt="Mela" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1492597212p2/41757430.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2626091371?book_show_action=true">Dec 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mela" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mela" href="/user/show/41757430-mela">Mela</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/26216013-venetia">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41757430-mela?shelf=british-literature">british-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41757430-mela?shelf=historical-romance">historical-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41757430-mela?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41757430-mela?shelf=zz-georgette-heyer">zz-georgette-heyer</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41757430-mela?shelf=historical-period-long-19th-century">historical-period-long-19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41757430-mela?shelf=my-hero">my-hero</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41757430-mela?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2626091371" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17043952944905890844">This was my first book by G. Heyer. I read it in my first year of reading in English, so I wasn't an admirer of English language yet (I knew it not enough). Now, after a second reading I admit, I underestimated it. I mean, 4 stars? Really? ;-)<br/><br/>Let's start with a general outline:<br/><br/>* Venetia Lanyon (25) - beautiful (gorgeous), sensible, rather calm, intelligent (almost bluestocking, horror! ;-) ), witty.<br/>* Lord Jasper Damerel (38) - nice-looking but not handsome, intelligent (even bookish), witty, a r</span>
  <span id="freeText17043952944905890844" style="display:none">This was my first book by G. Heyer. I read it in my first year of reading in English, so I wasn't an admirer of English language yet (I knew it not enough). Now, after a second reading I admit, I underestimated it. I mean, 4 stars? Really? ;-)<br /><br />Let's start with a general outline:<br /><br />* Venetia Lanyon (25) - beautiful (gorgeous), sensible, rather calm, intelligent (almost bluestocking, horror! ;-) ), witty.<br />* Lord Jasper Damerel (38) - nice-looking but not handsome, intelligent (even bookish), witty, a rake but sensible, rather calm.<br /><br />Now, some information from my ratings (database) of Heyer's novels:<br /><br />--&gt; It is, first of all, a romance.<br />--&gt; Jasper is 'my hero' - I mean, he stops and speeds up my heartbeat, I am comparing him to Rochester ("Jane Eyre") and as for now, Jasper wins.<br />--&gt; A funny, charming first scene.<br />--&gt; A memorable (romantic and witty) last scene.<br />--&gt; A fantastic, memorable first meeting.<br />--&gt; Swooning, romantic love scenes.<br />--&gt; At least one surprising plot twist.<br />--&gt; Words that I will remember: 'my dear delight', 'greengirl', 'idiotish'.<br />--&gt; Some other characters really well written: Aubrey, Mrs. Scorrier, Mrs. Hendred, Oswald, Edward Yardley, Nurse, Marston.<br /><br />Now, a look:<br /><br /><i>“Do you imagine he would be faithful to you?”<br />“I don't know,” said Venetia. “I think he will always love me. You see, we are such dear friends.”</i><br /><br /><i>“Do you think you will make me unhappy?”<br />“I don't—but I will offer you no promises!”<br />“No, pray don't!” she said seriously. “As soon as one promises not to do something it becomes the one thing above all others that one most wishes to do!” She turned her head towards her uncle again. “You mean to warn me that he may continue to have mistresses, and orgies, and—and so-on, don't you sir?”<br />“Particularly so-on!” interpolated Damerel.<br />“Well, how should I know all the shocking things you do? The thing is, uncle, that I don't think I ever should know.”<br />“You'd know about my orgies!” objected Damerel.<br />“Yes, but I shouldn't care about them, once in a while. After all, it would be quite unreasonable to wish you to change all your habits, and I can always retire to bed, can't I?”<br />“Oh, won't you preside over them?” he said, much disappointed.<br />“Yes, love, if you wish me to,” she replied, smiling at him. “Should I enjoy them?”<br />He stretched out his hand, and when she laid her own in it, held it very tightly. “You shall have a splendid orgy, my dear delight, and you will enjoy it very much indeed!”</i><br /><br />There is no need for more recommendations. Truly, 'Venetia' is <b>a perfection of Regency romance</b>. I would call that a few other Heyer's novels too (but I will refrain from doing it until the second read). And I completely agree with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/596363541?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne's enthusiasm and review</a>. [Warning: In Anne's review are spoilers.]<br /><br />By the way, I would like to read a story about Venetia and Jasper's children, how they, as parents, would have behaved when their child fell in love etc. Can you imagine it? Jasper as a father of a girl having encounters with some young rake, sensible Venetia trying to pacify Jasper. ;-) Go ahead, daydream with me ;-)</span>
  <a data-text-id="17043952944905890844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
